عليه وسلم : نعم أيها الملك » مى اردت ذلك مفابعت الى عانى ان شاء الله فاعل ٠‏ 
فال : إن آحبه الى وأوففه اأعيحله » ۰ 


فأوحى الى يوسف صل الله عليه وسلم أن تحفر بلانة خليج : خليجا من آعلى الصعيد 
من موصعح لذا الى موضح كذا › وحلیجا شرقيا من موضع ددا الى موضع كذا » وخليجا 
عربيا من موضع كذا الي موضح لدا » فوصع يوسف عليه السلام العمال فحفر خليج 
امنهى من على اشمون الى اللاهون » وآمر ابناتين أن يحغروا اللامون » وحفر حليج 
اعيوم » وهو الخليج الشرقى ٠‏ وحفر حليجا بفریه قال لها : تنهمت من فرى اعيوم٠‏ 
رسو الحليج الخغربى › فخرج ماؤها من الحليج الشرقى فصب فى النيل › وخرج من 
الحليج الغربى فصب فى صحراء ننهمت الى الغرب ٠‏ فلم يبق فى الجوبة ماء ٠‏ بم 
ادحدها الفعله فقطع ما دان فيها من القصب والطرغاء وأخرجه منها » وكان ذلك إابتداء 
جرى اليل وقد صارت الجوبه أرضا ريفية بريه ٠‏ وارتغع ماء الئيل فدخل فى راس 
المنهى فجرى فيه حتى النتهى الى اللاهون ففطعه الى الفيوم ٠‏ فدخل خليجها فسقاها 
فصارت مجه من النيل › فخرح اليها الملك ووزراؤه وكان هذا كله فى سبعين يوما ٠‏ 
فلما نظر اليها الملك قال لوزرائه أولئك : هذا عمل آلف بوم ٠‏ فسميت الفيوم ٠‏ 
رأقامت آنزرع كما تزرع غوابط عضر °

قال : ثم رجع الى حدیث عشمان وفیره قال : 

د فماقيت مصر بعد غرقهم ليس فيها من أشراف آهلها أحد » ولم ببق بها الا 
العبيد والاجراء والنساء ٠‏ خاعظم آشراف من بمصر من النساء أن يولين منهم أحدا 
وأجمع رأيهن آن يولين امرآة منهن يقال لها دإوكة ابنة زباء » وكان لها عقل ومعرفة 
وتجارب وكائت فى شرف منهن وموضع › وعى يومثذ بدت مائة سئة وستيل سئه 
فملكوها فخافت إن يتناولها ملوك الارض فجمعت نساء الاشراف غقالت لهن : ان 
بلادنا لم يكن يطمم فيها أحد » ولا يمد عينه اليها » وقد هلك كابر نا وأشرافنا » وذهب 
السحرة الذين كنا نقوی بهم > وقد رایت آن آبنی حصنا أحدق به جميع بلاد نا فأضع 
عليه المحارس من كل ناحية فالا لا نأمن أن بيطمع فينا الناس ٠‏ فيثت جدارا أحاطت به 
على جميع أرض مصر كلها » المزارع والمداثن والقرى وجعلت دونه خليجا بجرى فيه 
الماء وأقامت القناطر والترع » وجعلت فيه محارس ومسالح على كل ثلائة ميال محرس 
ومسلحه » وفیما بین ذلك حارس صغار على کل ميل ۰ وجعلت فی کل حرس رجالا 
واجرت عليهم الارزاق › دامر تهھم آن بحر سوا بالاجر اس »ء غاذا اتاهم أحد بخافو نه 
صرب نعضهم ا دعەں بالا جر اس فاتاهم ار هن أی وحه کان › کی ساعة واحدة » 
فدظروا فى ذلك › فمنعت بذلك مصر ممن آرادها » ٠‏

ذلك آهل مصر صالموا الروم على أن یدفعوا الیھم شیئا مسمی خی کل عام على آن 
پمنعوهم ویکو نوا فی ذمتهم ۰ ثم ظهرت فارس على الروم فلما غلبوهم على الشام 
رغبوا فی مصر وطمعوا فیها فامتنح آهل مصر وأعانتهم الروم وقامت دو نهم واالدت 
عليهم فارس فلما خشوا ظهورهم علیهم صالوا فارس عل أن کون ما صالو! به 
الردم بين الروم وفارس ٠‏ فرضيت الروم بذلك حين خافت ظهور غارس عليها » فكان

عبد الرحمن بن عبد القارى قال : 

« حا مضی حاطب بکتاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ٠‏ قبل المفوقس الكتاب 
وآکرم حاطبا وآحجسن نزله > ٿم سر حه الى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم » وآهدی له 
مع حاطب كسوة وبغلة بسرجها وجاريتين احداعما آم ابراهيم » ووهب الاخرى لهم 
ابن قيس العبدرى فهى آم زكرياء بن جهم الذى كان خليفة عمرو بن العماص على مصر 
وبقال : بل وهبها لحسان بن ثابت فهى آم عبد الرحمن بن حسان ويقال : بل وهسها

« ان المغوقس لا تاه كتاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ضمه الى صدره وقال : 
هذا زمان يخر غيه اللبى الذى نجد لعته وصفته فى كتاب الله » وانا لمنجد صفته 
انه لا یجمع بین اختین فی ملك مین ۰ ولا نكأح ٠‏ وانه يقبل الهدية ولا قبل الصدقة 
وان جلساءه امسا کین » وان خانم النبوۃ بین کنفیه ء ثم دعا رجلا عاقلا » ثم لم پدرع صر 
آحسن ولا آجمل من مارية وأختيها وعما من آهل حفن من كورة انصنا فبعث هما ای

مہ وعسلا من عسل بنها » وبعث اليه بال صدقة » دأمر رسوله آن ينظ من جلساز.

و ان المسلمين لا حاصررا بابليون وكان به جماعة من الروم وآكابر القبطل 
ورؤساؤهم وعليهم المقوقس فقاتلوهم بها شهرا »› خلما رآى القوم الجد منهم على نتحه 
وا رص ورآوا من صبرهم على القتال ورغبتهم فيه › خافوا أن يظهر وا عليهم › فقنحی

والا برده الى بلدہ ولا الى أرضه ولا الى اأهله وولده » ولیس لاحد منا هم فيما خلفه 
وقد استودع كل واحد منا ربه أهله وولده وانما همنا ما أمامنا ٠‏ وما قولكانا

ما أردنا منها لانفسنا أكثر مما نحن عليه ٠‏ فانظر الذى تربد فبينه ليا فليس بيننا

الواحد 4# 
دساو ىمائة! 


« وشرط المقوقس للروم أن يحروا » فمن أحب منهم أن يقيم على مثلى هذا اقام 
على ذلك لازما له مفترضا عليه ممن أقام دالاسکددر رة وما حولھا من آرض مصر کلہها 
ومن اراد الحروج منها ال رض الروم حرج رع أن اللمقوقس الحیار فی الروم خاصة 
حتى يكتب الى ملك الروم يعلمه ما فعل فان قبل ذلك ورضيه جاز عليهم والا کانوا 
جميعا على ما كانوا عليه ٠‏ وكتبوا به كتابا وكثب المقوقس الى ملك الروم كتابا 
يعلمه على وجه الامر كله فكتب اليه ملك الروم يقح ريه وبعجزه ویرد عليه مافعل 
ويقول فى كتابه : انما آتاك من العرب اثنا عشر الفا ويمصر من بها من كثرة عدد 
القعل ما لا يحصى فان كان القبط كرهوا القتال وأحبوا آداء الجزية الى العرب 
واختاروهم علينا فان عندك بمصر من الروم بالاسكندريةه ومن معك آكثر من ماثة 
آلف معهم العدة والقوة » والعرب وحالهم وضعفهم على ما قد رأپت فعجزت عن قتالهم 
ورضیٹ آن کون أنت ومن معك من الروم فى حال القبط آذلاء ٠‏ ألا تقاتلهم أنت ومن 
معك من الروم حتى تموت أو تظهر عليهم فانهم فيكم على قدر كثرتكم وقوتكم وعل 
قدر تلتهم و ضعفهم کاكلة فناعضهم القشال ولك کون لك رآی غار ذلك ۰ و كشب 
ملك الروم بمشل ذلك كتابا الى جماعة الروم ٠‏ فقال المقوقس نا ١آتاه‏ كتاب ملك 
الروم : والله انهم على قاتهم وضعفهم أقوى وأشد منا على كثرتنا وقوتنا » ان الرجل

فخرج عقبة بن نافع سريعا بحنقه على أبى المهاجر » حتى قدم آفريقية فأوثق 
ابا المهاجر فی داق شدید » وأساء عزله » وغزا به معه الى السوس » وهو فی حدید » 
وبل السوس بطن من البربر يقال لهم أنبيه ٠‏ فجول فى بلادهم ٠‏ لا يعرض له أحد 
ولا بقاتله » فانصرف الي افريقية » فلما دنا من تغرعا أمر أصحابه فافترقوا » وأذن

و واغارت الأریم بعد حسان على انطابلس » فهرب ابراحيم بن النصرالى › 
وخلی آهل انطابلس واهل ذمتها فی ایدى الروم فرااسوها آربعين ليله » حتى آسرعوا 
فيها الفساد » وبلغ ذلك عبد العزيز بن مروان فارسل الى زهير بن قيس وكا حرج 
مع حسان » غلما بلغ مصر آقام بها فأمره عبد العزين بالنهوض الى الروم ٠‏ ولم يجتمع 
لڙهير من أصحابه الا يعون رجلا » وكان عارض من الصدف ٠‏ يقال له : جندل بن 
صخر » وکان خظا غليظا ۰ فقال زهر لعبد العزیز بن مروان : آما اذ قد آمرتنى 
باروج غلا تبعثن معی جندلا عارضا فیحبس على الناس لشدته وفظاظنه » وان 
عبد العزیز عاتبا على زحیر بن قيس » لانه کان قاتله حین وجهه آبوه مروان بن المحکم 
من ناحية أبلة من قبل أن يدخل مصر ٠‏ فقال له : ما علمتك يا زهير الا جلفا جافيا ٠‏

فقال : لا أرى له عقوبة ولا مكافأة الا أن أدخل عليه العرب › فبعث الى طارق انى 
مدخلك الاندلس » وطارق بومثد تلمسیين وموسی بن تصر بالقروان ٠‏ فقال طارق : 
فانى لا أطمشن اليك حتى تبعث الى برهيدة » فبعث البه بابنتيه ٠‏ ولم يكن له ولد 
غبرھما ۰ فاقرهما طارق بتلمسین › واسنوثق منھما ۰ تم خرج طارق الى پلیان وهو 
بسبتة على المجاز ففرح به حين قدم عليه » وقال له : أنا مدخلك الاندلس ٠‏ وكاب فيما 


بين المجازين جبل ٠‏ يقال له اليوم : جبل طارق فيما بين سسبته والاندالس ؛ فلما 
أمسی جاءه يليان بالمراكب » فحمله غيها الى ذلك المجاز » فاكمن فيه نهاره » فلما سى 
رد المراكب الى من بقى من أصحابه » فحملوا اليه حتى لم يبق منهم أحد ٠‏ رلا إشعر 
بهم أهل الاندلس ؛ ولا بظنون الا أن المراکب تختلف بمشثل ما کانت تخثلف به من 
متافعهم > وکان طارق فی آخر فوج رکب » فجاز الى أصحابه : وتخلف يليان ومن

على قنطرة من الجبل الى قرية يقال لها : قرطاجنة ٠‏ وزحف يريد قرطبة ٠‏ فر 
بجزبرة فى البحر فخلف بها جارية له يقال لها : أم حكيم ومعها نفر من جنده ›

« لما وجه اليهم المسلمون عمدوا الى ميناء لهم في البحر » فسدوه وآخرجوا 
منه المأاء » ثم قذفوا فيه آنيتهم من الذهب والفضة › ثم ردوا عليه الماء بحاله » وعمدرا

عليه الماء » فوقعت رجله على شىء خأخرجه فاذا صحفة من فضة » ثم غاص أيضاا 
فارج شيشا آخر » فلما علم المسلمون بذلك حبسوا عنه الماء ٠‏ وأخذوا جميع تلك 
الآئية ٠‏ ودخل رجل من المسلمين ومعه قوس بندق الى تلك الكنيسة التى رفعوا بين 
سقفيها مالهم » فنظر الى حمام فرماه بيندقة فأخطاه » وأماب شبحة خشب فكسرها 
وانهال عليهم المال فغل المسلمون يومئذد غلولا كشرا ٠‏ فان كان الرجل لباخذ الهر 
فیڈبحھا ویرمی بما فی جوفها تم یحشوه مما غل ثم یخیط عليه ویرمی بها ال‌الطریق 
ليتوهم من رآها انها ميثتة فاذا حرج آخذها › وان کان الرجل نزع نصل سيغه 
فيطرحه ويملا الجفن غلولا ويضع قائم السيف على الجن ٠‏ فلما ركبوا السقن وتو هوا 
سمعوا منادیا بنادی : اللهم غرق بهم فتقلدوا المصاسحف فغرقوا جميعا الا آيو شد

« فأراد عبد الله بن عبد الملك عرزل ابن حدیع » فاستحیی من عزله عن غير شیء

24° INTRODUCTION 


through Mr. Ellis, the task, at that time precarious, of getting the parcels 
of manuscript safely to the printers in Leyden. 

In editing the text, the readings of Ms. A have been preferred wherever 
they could reasonably be regarded ge the original. The same principles of 
vocalization hare been observed as those adopted by the editors of Fabari’s 
great work. The notes, which might easily have been considerably increased 
in volume, are intended to supply only what is most necessary. There is 
probably no need to apologize for the amount of space giren to the glossary. 
The great age and comparative certainty of the text, the influence which 
the book exerted in the learned Muslim world, and the need of sifted 
material, of definite date and undoubted authenticity, for an Arabic dictionary 
compiled on scientific principles, constitute a sufficient excuse for discussing 
unusual words at some length, and for including usage already recorded in 
Dozy’s Supplénent (generally, it is true, from works much later in date 
than the Futüh Mişr). The reader who compares our text with the extensive 
excerpts from it given by Suyütl, Maqrîzî, and other writers of the later 
period will be interested, And perhaps surprised, to see how often the 
worde and phrases listed in this glossary are either omitted altogether or 
else replaced by others which are more usual. The reader is urged to 
consult the table of Brrata, before making use of the book, and to enter 
in the text the correotions there indicated. 

The editor takes this opportunity to thark those who have assisted, in 
one way or another, in the preparation of this edition: the officers of the 
Bibliothèque Nationale, the British Museum, and the Library of the University 
of Leyden, for the aid so readily aud unreservedly given; tho Trustees of 
the E. J. W. Gibb Mernorial Fund, for their uniform courtesy and consideration ; 
the managers of ' the Yale University Press, and the publishing house of 
E. J. Brill in Leyden, for the care which they have bestowed on all the 
details of printing and publication; Mr. A. J. Ellis, formerly of the British 
Museum, afterward of the India Office, for lıis unfailing helpfulness in many 
ways; Professor Snouck Hurgronje, of the University of Leyden, for generously 
offering to read a proof of the work, and for some valuable corrections of 
the text which JI have included, aceompanied by his name, in the table of 
Hrrata. To my colleague, Professor Clay, to whose energy the inception 
of the Yale Oriental Series is due, J am indebted for much help and 
encouragement. Dr. George Alexander Kohut, of New York City, has shown 
@ keen personal interest in the publication of this volume, and it is especially 


fitting that it should appear under the auspices of the fund established in 
memory of his father, COiiRiEk O FORRES 
New Haven, Conn., September, 1921.

Since the present edition has been often promised and announced, during 
the past thirty years, and especially since it was for some time announced 
ag one of the publications to be issued in the “HB. J. W. Gibb Memorial”

bogun in 1805, with the copying and photographing of the greater part of 
the older Daris manuscript. In another summer vacation in 1898 this tran- 
script was completed, and the collation of Diss. C and A. (made known to 
me in the meantime by Rieu’s Catalogue) was accomplished. In 1901 a 
visit to Leyden made possible the collation of Ms. D. Some years later, 
when the ‘Trustees of the Cibb Fund offered to publish the work, the text 
and notes were still based on As. B, but it seemed important to revise the 
wliole on the basis of the text of A. Other labors, which could neither be 
neglected nor postponed, prevented, however. In March, 1914, the last in- 
stallment of tho revised text and notes, in final form, was sent to London. 
Before tha printing could be begun, however, the Buropean war, c9mbined 
with an unusual and unexpected drain on the resources of the Fund, ren» 
dered publication in the uear future unlikely. At about this tine the Yale 
Oriental Series was instituted, and, glad to publish under its auspices if 
arrangements could bo made, I suggested to the Gibb trustees in 1916 the 
transfer of the material. They very courteously consented, and undertook,

form, without mention of their source. One of the very early histories of 
which the Arabic text has been lost, but which is preserved in translation,

a work of which we are told that the original text was edited by aa-Silafî.

from Mr. A. G. Ellis.

ration and their immediate successors, to drop out of sight, Bven after it 
was rescued from obliyion, and its material began to’ ba used extensively 
in other works, the discredit attaching to the name of its author seems to 
have lingered for some time, ‘The way in which al-Kindî, in writing of the 
qidîs of Egypt, makes frequent and direct use of the Futih Migr, while 
avoiding the appearance of doing 40, is highly significant. Fe bases his 
treatise on that of his predecessor even to tho extent of making it end 
with the year 246, and yet, as Guest remarks (Introduction, p. 24), in 
using the same traditions he prefers not to cite Ibn “Abd al-Hakam, but 
instead employs, wherever possible, another chain of authorities, We may 
suppose that not long after the death of our author Ibn Qudaid came into 
possession of a ma. of the Futübh Migr which was either the work of one 
of the pupils of the discredited historian or else the copy of such a work, 
He had nothing more authoritative with which fo oompare it and by which 
to correot it, and therefore handed it on as he found it, like a true rîîfwf, 
We have seen that the edition of the Futiüph Migr whioh was delivered 
by Ibn Qudaid to his own pupils, with his numerous brief additions and 
comments, was tranamitted from him by Mulkammad ibn Ahmad al-Qammih; 
and also, that in the older Paris manuscript it ia repeatedly naserted that 
its text was handed down from Ibn Qudaid by his more famous pupil, al- 
Kindf. The question of the authenticity of this latter information at once 
suggests itself, since the text of Ms. B is practically identical with that of 
the other witıesses, and we have no other direct testimony that al-Kindi 
was concerned with the transmission of this work. Jf is intrinsically pro» 
bable, however, that this should have been the case, seeing that his teacher 
and ohief authority (Guest, Introd., p: 18) gave out an edition of it ' with 
his own comments and slight additions. It is a work of just the sort which 
would be most likely to interest al-KindT; we know that he was acquainted

It remains to find probable answers to the questions, how [bn Qudaid 
came into possession of this one faulty codex, and why he transmitted it 
to his pupils in this imperfect state, without correction. It is to be observed, 
in the first place, that there is no good evidence that Ibn Qudaid was ever 
4 pupil of Ibn “Abd al-Hakam or received traditions from him orally. The 
best native biographical treatises do not claim this for him, and in the few 
cases where the assertion is made it igs undoubtedly due simply to his trans- 
mission of the I"utüh Migr. His attitude, throughout this work, is plainly 
that of an editor who occasionally adds his own marginal notes (such as 
those given İn note 16 on pp. 107 f., in 247, 15 ff, 800, 14 f., etc.), rather 
than that of a pupil transmitting a work received from his master. There 
is a noticeable absence of any indication that he had himself heard Ibn 
‘Abd al-Hakam; in the one passage where thia has the appearance of being: 
the case, namely 63, 7 and note 10, where Ibn “Abd al-Hakam is said to 
have prescribed the reading 4م‎ ql instead 0f gol, the explanation is 
furnished by the parallel passage, p. 280, in which “Abd ar-Rahmüãn remarks 
(lines 16 f.) that Abü-’l-Aswad, from whom he received the tradition, used 
to pronounce the name with final im '. It was in the year 237, when Ibn 
Qudaild was eight years old, that the family of Ibn “Abd al-Hakam fell 
into the disgrace from which it never recovered. It is not likely that ‘Abd 
ar-Rahmaãn, who at that time was about fifty years of ago, gained any new 
adherents after that date, but it may well be that a few of his former 
pupils remained by him. It would seem that after the death of the master 
his history waa not circulated, but was allowed, by those of his own gene-

statement of the writer of Ms. A (see above), that he had seen and coneulted 
a codex which had been read before Ibn Qudaid, and we can also be sure 
that he found its readings practically identical with those of the Silafî 
oodices. On the other hand, it is certain that [bn Qudaid did not receive 
his text of the Futuh Migr directly from Ibn “Abd al-Hakam, nor from 
any authoritative intermediate source. Some good fortune put into his hands 
the only surviving copy of this important work. The reasons for drawing 
this conclusion, and the grounds for conjecture as to the origin of this 
unique and faulty copy, will appear from the facts presently to be set forth. 

The evidence at hand seems to show that the manuscript which came 
into the possession of Ibn Qudaid had been carefully written, preserving 
faithfully in general the words of Ibn “Abd al-Hakam. Bither this manuscript, 
however, or one from which it was derived had been haudled carelcsaly, so 
that chapter-headings were either omitted or misplaced; marginal notes or 
corrections, and longer supplementary paragraphs, were not always inserted 
in the right place; and in one case the accidental misplacement of a leaf 
had led to the transposition and verbal repetition of extended passages. 

Ã striking example of misplaced supplementary matter is to be seen 
in the two addenda to Juz’ I, pp. 43, 18—44, 17; see the footnotes there. 
They are utterly out of place where they stand, have no connection with 
each other, and contaln no direct indication of the contexts to which they 
belong. They were probably additions made on loose pieces of paper. 
A similar example is the paragraph at the foot of p. 145, at the end of 
the chapter entitled aa Jq jS. It has no connection whatever with either 
the preceding or the following context, and doubtless orjçinally stood at 
the end of the chapter entitled .م ,لكر القطاتع‎ 139, to. ts displacement 
Was probably connected with the. accidental transovosition of leaves in 
pP. 133-—189, already mentioned. An excellent example of a misplaced 
clause, found in all our mss. and attested elsewhere as the reading of the 
Futüh Mişr, is p. 14, lines 15 f.; see note 14. Another instance, even more 
striking, ia shown on p. 201, note 12. A most interesting illustration of a 
gloss inserted in tho wrong place is 172, 1 f.; see note Š5, We find the 
same false order in YAaqüt, who quotes the passage, and, what is more,

CHossary 6.¥. yyw. (What is true in this case seems to bo true everywhere 
else: the only known text of the Futüh Mişr is the text of Ibn Qudaid.)

i" INTRODUCTION 


itsclf, that A and D represent a line of descent in whioh the original form 
of the chapter has been preserved, while B and C represent a different 
line in which the accidental corruption had taken place, cannot be accepted 
aa the frue explanation. In the first place, B and C do not otherwise bear 
the marks of such an immediate common origin; secondly, there is the 
very early date of B, joined to the fact that Û is a manuscript of the 
Silafî text; and thirdly, the facts which will presently be set forth render 
another explanation virtually certain, namely, that the superior text of A 
and D at this point is the result of editing. We have seen that the Futüh 
Migr received considerable study in Egypt in the sixth century A.H. Some 
learned Muslim of Alexandria ~— whether the writer of A, or of the 
ancestor of D (about the year 510), or of some other codex — rectificd 
the very obvious and disturbing blunder in the chapter mentioned, and 
perhaps also made some of the improvements in the orthography which are 
not only conspicuous in A but also show their traces in D. That this was 
not as-Silafl himself appears from the fact that in the ancestor of Û, which 
also represents the Silafîi text, the revision had not taken place. 

Study of the evidence afforded by our four manuscripts of the Futiüh 
Migr reveals a very interesting and somewhat surprising fact, namely, that 
the text of Ibn Qudaid, from which all our witnesses come, was derived 
from a single faulty codex, whose ınanifestly defective and disarranged text 
he had no means of rectifying, or rather, no authority to rectify. It cannot 
seriously be doubted that the remarkably uniform text which woe have 
Lefora us is in reality what it professes to be, the text of Ibn Qudaid, 
transmitted from him with care by the four scholars who are named as 
having vouched for its accuracy. We have no reason to question the

8° INTRODUCTION 


Salil, he is known ae one of the Egyptian authorities who could quote 
exactly from memory a great body of historical tradition (see Guest, p. 26 f.); 
nevertheless the way in which he is oited by Ibn “Abd al-akam suggests 
that some, at least, of his material was available in written form. ' 

‘Abd ar-Rahmîn's especial interest in the QEdis of Egypt, whose history, 
carried down to the last decade of his own life, is sketched in the sixth 
juz’, i doubtless due to the intimate connection of his family with this 
branch of the Muslim administration. His father and brothers, especially 
Muhammad, were noted jurists, as has already been observed, and his futher, 
‘Abdallah, had been associated with the qadî as censor of witnesses, “Abd 
ar-Rakmãn shows himself chiefly concerned with the period of the Companions 
and their immediate successors; hence in his treatment of the qidis he gives 
much space to the earliest, but less and less to the later ones down to the 
time of his writing. The material of the seventh juz’, containing the traditions 
preserved in Egypt from the Companions who entered the land, is of course 
given in strictly conventional form. The principal authority here, as has 
already been said, is Ibn Lahi‘a. Some of the minor rãwîs in the isnãds 
are of doubtful authority, but the collection as a whole is interesting and 
instructive. Not a few of the traditions recorded here have already appeared 
in earlier chapters of the book, in various connections. Attention ig usually, 
though not always, called’ to this fact in the footnotes. 

Ibn ‘Abd al-Hakam occasionally criticizes his sources, a8 any expert in 
tradition might; see for example 64, 9; 231, 11 ff.; 239, 1T f.; 265, 14f; 
295, Ilf; 801, 18fE; 810, note 2; but in general it cannot be said that 
he shows great ability as a historian, either in his selection of material or 
İn his treatment of it. Nevertheless he has given us a most valuable 
collection of the Egyptian memoranda, producing a book which not only 
was Yery widely used itself, but also served as the atarting point and basis 
of a number of Egyptian historical works of high importance. 

The manuscripts of the Futüh Mişr now known to be in existence are 
four in number, of which one is in London in the British Museum, two 
are in the Bibliothèque Nationale in Paris, and the fourth is in the library 
of the University in Leyden. ° The following is a brief desoription of them.

The form of the tradition with full isnšds is of course generally em- 
ployed throughout the work. As has already been sald, this form of citation 
does not necessarily imply the use of oral sources. Tho material at Ibn 
‘Abd al-Hakam's disposal, partly written and partfy oral, was certainly 
abundant, but slao very miscellaneous, consisting in considerable part of 
popular tales and legends. It does not appear that any oro of his predecessors 
had been capable of sifting the mass of testimony with the instinct of a 
historian, recognizing the things which were of chief importance and making 
thorough search for the facts while they could still be ascertained. What 
criticism there was had been applied mainly to the chains of tradition, and 
even this had apparently not been carried very far. The rawî through whom 
the largest part of Ibn “Abd al-Hakam’s material had come (as algo, later, 
much of al-Kindi’s; Guest, p. 32) was Ibn Lahi“a (f 174), a collector who 
seems always to hare placed quantity above quality, besides being notoriously 
untrustworthy. His very voluminous collections had been put in writing, 
largely if not wholly; see above. As a matter of course, the famous Egyptian 
authority, al-Laith ibn Sad (f 175), is very extensively drawn upon in every 
part of the work. Though far more reliable as a rãwî than Ibn Lahia, the 
vulue of his collections by no means corresponds to their bulk. We know 
that much of his material had been reduced to writing. A. traditionist of 
value for the early history of Mohammedan Egypt is the son of a Nubian 
freedman, Yazid ibn Abî Habîb (f 128), and ‘the Futüh Misr, which cites 
him very often, has profited by his industry. A book compiled by Yahya 
ibn “Abdallah ibn’ Bukair (f 231), from which our author tells us that he 
took material, contained the transcript of letters and similar documenta (see 
160, 9f), and doubtless much besides that was valuable for such a history 
ag this, Ibn Bukair had been at some pains to record the dates of events 
(generally obtained from al-Laith ibn Sad), and those contained in the 
Futuh Migr are very largely given as from him. 

In the chapter dealing with the Khittas, isnãds rarely appear, since the 
material consisted mainly of information derived from popular tradition — 
the common property of the people of al-Fustag — supplemented by the 
author's own information. A considerable part of this interesting and important

under the former, and of Nubia and a portion of North Africa under the

the moat extensive of all the divisions, contains 4 selection of religious tra- 
ditiong derived from those Companions of the Prophet who came to Bgypt, 
namely such traditions as are distinctively Egyptian and recognized as such 
among those loarned in this science. Fifty-two Companions are named, 
beginning with “Amr and his son “Abdallah, and under each name those 
trnditions are given which are regarded as well attested. These are followed 
by a few anecdotes handed down, in Egypt only, from fourteen other Com- 
panions; and these in turn by incidents reported from three others whose 
sojourn in Egypt is knowa only through the traditionists of other countries. 
Finally those (seven in numbcr) are named from whom neither tradition 
nor narrative is reported; together with still others (nine) who are said by 
Waqidî and other authorities to have entered the land. Thus Iba ‘Abd 
al-Hakam in his seventh Book takes some account of every member of the 
SalhRba who is credibly declared to have set foot in Egypt.

classify tho voluminous Muslim tradition relating to Egypt. Others before 
him had made less ambitious collections; how much these included, and to 
what extent the material was already classified, we have no means of knowing, 
but it is evident that some ^f them were of very considerable volume and 
importance. The sources used by “Abd ar-Rahman were in part purely oral, 
for there were many in Egypt —~ his father “Abdallxh was one — who 
prided themselves on their ability to report from memory a large body of 
hadıth in tho time-honored way, and the frequent dya and gîz in the 
Futah Migr can be taken at their face value. On the other hand, it is 
certain that a very large part of his material was derived from written 
collectiona. The distinction between the two modes of transmission, oral and 
written, cannot be sharply drawn, to be sure, for many authorities had been 
wont to write down, for convenience, the single traditions or the extended 
works which they at the same time held perfectly in memory; what already 
stood in a book might be, and still usually was, handed on by word of 
mouth. The Futuh Migr itself was presumably dictated by “Abd ar-Rahman 
to companies of his hearers, assembled for the purpose; and he had üun- 
doubtedly written much from similar dictation, in addition to perusing care- 
fully the manuscript works of some of his chief authorities. He of course 
makes reference to persons, not to writings; we do, however, find in one

ibn Bukair — one of his principal authorities — which had been given to

his own manuscript material, but there is some evidence that this alao waa 
the copy of a document, rather than the record of oral tradition, inasmuch 
as it is reported as from Ibn Lahi'a, who died before our author was born, 
and whose materials were available in written form. In more than one place 
where “Abd ar-Rahmin expresses his own doubt as to a word in the hadith 
which he is reporting, and gives an alternative, the ground of the uncer« 
tainty is very obviously graphic. See 255, 20 (gS and j5J) and 291, 22

Lahi'a, who is generally believed, on good evidence, to have coınpiled books

seveuth division of his work, reports directly from Ibn Lahi'a without any

In the year 237 the family met with a disaster in whiol it lost per- 
manently its reputation and influence. The acèount of the matter is given 
in al-Kindî, I, 1l4—f., 2; IN, 13—., 11; fo, 13—-16; Fr, 15" و4‎ ° 
FI, d4—fh, 12; fv, 1—5; space is given to it here because it seems fo 
have had an important bearing on the history of the transmission of the 
Futuh Migr and its material. The very considerable property of the former 
governor and military leader “Alî ibn Abd al-“Azîz al-Jarawî was confiscated 
by the government; in the meantime several of the prominent men of Egypt, 
and foremost among them the Banü “Abdallah ibn “Abd al-Hakam, had taken 
temporary charge of the estate. When at length the emissaries of the caliph 
al-lMlutawakkil arrived in Bgypt to claim the money for the state treasury, 
it was not to be found. Legal proceedings were instituted, and the decision 
against the Banü “Abdallah ibn “Abd al-Hakam was for the sum of 1,404,000 
dînîrs (Kindf, ff, 12). In the course of the proceedings one of the brothers, 
‘Abd al-Hakam, died under torture. The others were soon released from 
prison, and such property as rightfully belonged to the family was restored; 
but the fall of the once honored house was complete. “You have strange 
ways of dealing in your courts of law, here in Egypt,” said a man of “Irfq, 
#ome time after these events; “you give weight to the testimony of this 
negro slave Sulaim, while such a renowned lawyer as Muhammad ibn “Ab 
dallah ibn “Abd al-Hakam is discredited as a witness (جتروح)‎ Whereupon 
Sulaim, overhearing, exclaimed: “J, at' any rate, never proved false to my 
trust, nor laid claim to what was not my own!’ (Kindî fv, 1 ff.).

INTRODUCTION 


The Futiükh Migr of Ibn ‘Abd al-Hakam is the earliest surviving account, 
from Arab sources, of the Mohammedan conquest of Bgypt and tho West, 
the first Arab settlements in and about al-Fustat and Alexandria, and allied 
topica concerning the occupation and early history of the land under the 
rule of Islam. The text here presented is published for the first time, on 
the authority of all the known manuscripts. 

Tho author of the work, “Abd ar-Rahmûãn ibn “Abdallah ibn “Abd al- 
Hakam ibn Ayan ibn DLaith, Abu '[]-Qasim al-Qurašîl, was born about 187 
A.H. (Tahdhîb at-Tahdkhib), and died at al-Fustat in the year 257 (A.D. 871). 
Ho was thus a contemporary of Beltdhuri (f 279) and Tabarî (fF 310), and 
a pioneer in the period in which the first comprehensive Mohammedan 
histories were constructed from the unwieldy mass of oral and written 
tradition. On the author and his work see Ibn Hajar, Tahdhîb at-Tahdhîb 
(Ifaidarabad 1326), VI 208; Dozy, Recherches, 3° éd., 36 ff; Wiüstenfeld, 
Geschichtschreiber n°. 6¥; Ewald, Zeitschrift fir die Kunde des Morgenlandes 
III 3 (1840), 829—-8523; Do Slane, Journal Asiatique 1844, pp. 330, 348, 
351, 354 ff.; Rhuvon Guest, Governors and Judges of Egypt, Introduction, 
pp. 22 f. Also Ibn Khallikîn, ne 322, 651 (Trans. Slane, I1 14, 508); 
Suyüftl, Husn al-Mufadara (lithogr.), I 134, 136, 206; Abi 'l-Mahisin I 
629; Hixujjîi alifa IV, p. 886; Brockelmann, C@eschichte der arab, Litteratur, 
I 148, II 692; The article “Ibn “Abd al-Hakam” in the Encyclopaedia of 
Islam; and the editions and translations of portions of the Futüph Migr 
mentioned below. 

Ibn Abd al-Hakam waa by training and inclination rather an expert 
authority in the soionco of tradition than a historian. The family of which 
ho was a member was renowned in ËÊgypt and abroad, in ita day, for its 
achievements in the various branches of hadîth and fiqh. The father of the 
family, “Abdallth ibn Abd al-Hakam, was one of the leading authorities in 
these fields. Himself a rkwî of high rank, he had also written books on 
tradition and jurisprudence, and was the head of the Malikite school in 
Egypt. The four sons of Abdallah were all men of importance: Muhammad,

TIIE ALEXANDER KOHUT MEMORIAL PUBLICATION FUND. 


The present volume is the third work published by the Yale University 
Press on tho Alexander Kohut Memorial Publication Fund. This Foundation 
was establislcd October 13, 1915, by a gift to Yale University from members 
of his family for the purpose of enabling scholars to publish texts and 
monographs in the Semitic field of research. 

Tho HRevercnd Alexander Kohut, Ph. D. (Leipzig), a distinguished 
Oriental scholar, in whose memory the fund has been established, was born 
in Ilungary, April 22, 1842, of a noted family of rabbis. When pastor 
of tho Congregation Alavath Chesed in New York City, he became one of 
the founders of the ‘Jewish Theological Seminary, and was a profcesor in 
that institution until his death. He was a noted pulpit orator, able to 
discourage with equal mastery in three languages. Among his contributions 
to Semitic learning is the monumental work Aruch Completum, an cnoy- 
clopacdic dictionary of the Talmud, in eight volumes, Semitic and Oriental 
scholara have honored lis memory by inscribing to him a volumo of Semitic 
Studies (Berlin, 1897),
